          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=childrens-books" class="actionLinkLite">childrens-books</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=inspirational" class="actionLinkLite">inspirational</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=read-with-wife" class="actionLinkLite">read-with-wife</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=first-reads" class="actionLinkLite">first-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1656440-hamster?shelf=british-authors" class="actionLinkLite">british-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer127686737">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13759636087517716147">I was in college the first time I read all the Chronicles of Narnia. Eight years later, I was ecstatic to get the whole set for free through the Goodreads first reader program. (Thanks to Harper Collins.) This time around I enjoyed them quite a bit more and understood the symbolism a little better. Rating on story alone I probably would give them three or four stars. But because all the stories are so deep in meaning and strike a chord with Christians everywhere, I've bumped the series up to AMA</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13759636087517716147">I was in college the first time I read all the Chronicles of Narnia. Eight years later, I was ecstatic to get the whole set for free through the Goodreads first reader program. (Thanks to Harper Collins.) This time around I enjoyed them quite a bit more and understood the symbolism a little better. Rating on story alone I probably would give them three or four stars. But because all the stories are so deep in meaning and strike a chord with Christians everywhere, I've bumped the series up to AMAZING.<br /><br />I've reviewed each book individually (in order.) Don't expect to find anything profound or insightful in any of my comments. I just recorded how the books made me feel and what I found particularly great (or not-so-great) about them.<br /><br /><strong>The Magician's Nephew</strong>, the first book in the Narnia series (which was actually the sixth published) receives 5 stars from me. (Here they are: * * * * *)<br />Although I read this book only a couple years ago to my wife, I found it just as enjoyable the second time. The first half of the book (Polly and Diggory's discovery of magic travel and struggles with the witch) is basically the main story, while the second half (Founding of Narnia) is a very long denouement. Although the falling action is full of annoying animal conversation and endless description of landscape, the symbolism is quite poignant. If the book was split into two I would give the first 5 stars and the second 4. But together the first half easily makes up for the meandering finish. Why do I love the beginning so much? Very clever writing, a terribly gripping plot (I've said before that the Wood between Worlds is the most exciting concept I've ever encountered in any book), and of course the great one liners. For example, "That's absolute bosh from beginning to end."<br /><br /><strong>The Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe</strong> is the most well known of the seven books, and it's one of my favorites. Even so, I grew a little bored reading this one, and I attribute it to the many readings and countless film adaptations I've ingested over the years. Unlike the Magician's Nephew, this has a great cohesive plot from beginning to end. And even if Aslan does come in at the end and save the day a little easily, the Christian symbolism is nicely done. Five stars.<br /><br /><strong>A Horse and His Boy</strong> was an incredibly engaging story from beginning to end. (5 stars) The symbolism of God helping us through our tough times brought me close to tears, and it was so satisfying to see justice served in the end. It took me until now to comprehend the cleverness of the title. (My mind always just switched the horse and boy around.) I'll end with one of my favorite lines uttered by King Lune: "Have we no more gravity among us than to be so chafed by the taunt of a pajock?"<br /><br /><strong>Prince Caspian</strong> was a great book (four stars), although in general I enjoyed the movie more. The duel between Miraz and Peter, however, was actually better in the book. Lewis has an incredible knack for making you feel like you're there watching. One of my favorite lines: <br />"That's the worst of girls," said Edmund to Peter and the Dwarf. "They never carry a map in their heads."<br />"That's because our heads have something inside them," said Lucy.<br />Something else that I found funny was the difference in language and meaning. This line for example would probably have to be cut from any of today's children's books: "Of course, if the children had attempted a journey like this a few days ago in England, they would have been knocked up."<br />One thing I didn't understand was this: The Pevensies are called into Narnia by the horn and then it takes them a few days to get to where Prince Caspian is (I think they sleep at least twice.) But then when they get there they hear Caspian saying he blew it just that morning. (p.391) Did I just read it wrong? Can someone explain this to me? <br /><br />After re-reading <strong>The Voyage of the Dawn Treader</strong> my hat goes off to the three screenplay writers that adapted this for film. Lewis's original is rich with originality and symbolism, but destitute in storyline. The movie somehow brought together all the episodic "lessons" and turned it into a cohesive whole. <br />Now I remember why this book wasn't one of my favorites. Not enough story to sink my teeth into. Still, it was an enjoyable read. I would probably get more out of the novels if I was astute enough to understand all the symbolism. With this book, I quite often said to myself, "I know that last story was supposed to mean something...oh well, let's see if anything cool happens on the next island."<br />The best line of the book was also the first: "There was a boy called Eustace Clarence Scrubb, and he almost deserved it."<br />Three stars for Voyage of the Dawn Treader. * * * And three cheers for me for getting through these so quickly with three kids and a full time job.<br /><br />I enjoyed <strong>The Silver Chair</strong> much more the second time. (4 stars) I'd remembered the Marsh-Wiggle as being exceptionally annoying. This time I found Puddleglum's constant pessimism exceptionally funny. (I'm not sure what that says about me now.) The plot was very well formed with a beginning, middle, and end (this one should convert very nicely to film.) I really liked the symbolism of the Queen trying to convince them there was no overland (akin to unbelievers trying to convince us there is no God or heaven.) <br />And I now know this is immature, but I just have to share a few more of the tidbits that sound funny in our modern culture. (Apologies to Clive Staples.) <br />"Gay," said Puddleglum with a deep sigh. "Thats what weve got to be. Gay." ... "All right. Gay's the word," said Scrubb. "Now, if we could only get someone to open this door. While were fooling about and being gay, weve got to find out all we can about this castle."<br />I'll finish with one more passage and just let you wonder what it's really about. (Hint: Lewis's intentions were rated G.) <br />She made love to everyone - the grooms, the porters, the housemaids, the ladies-in-waiting, and the elderly giant lords whose hunting days were past. She submitted to being kissed and pawed about by any number of giantesses, many of whom seemed sorry for her...<br /><br /><strong>The Last Battle</strong> started out with a great story, but then kind of threw the plot away to make room for the powerful end of the world allegory. Although I did appreciate Lewis's amazing insights into what the end of the world will be like (and the profound symbolism of Christ as Alsan), I would've also like to have seen the characters find a way out of their predicament without being magicked away. I guess in this last book at least, I can't satisfy both my need for story and need for meaningful symbolism. <br /><br />Overall, C. S. Lewis does an amazing job crafting stories that ring true to Christians and Story Lovers alike. The writing is good enough that you can choose to ignore all the deeper meanings. Why anyone would do that, however, makes as much sense to me as a Liberal watching Fox News to get the weather. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13759636087517716147">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_166589648">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/166589648" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4740880-book-elf" class="left imgcol" title="Book Elf"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1376298234p2/4740880.jpg" alt="Book Elf" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/166589648?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 22, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4740880-book-elf" class="user" title="Book Elf">Book Elf</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/4740880-book-elf?shelf=borrowed-books" class="actionLinkLite">borrowed-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer166589648">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17970933628451423984">When I first grabbed the book, my thought was, "Oh my! There's a whole lot to read." As you can see, I have watched the Chronicles of Narnia movies 1 to 3 (The Lion, The Witch and The Wardrobe; Prince Caspian; The Voyage of The Dawn Treader) before reading the book itself, and it was so thick!. So when I came across the 1st book, I was puzzled. I thought, "oh, so The Lion, The Witch and The Wardrobe was not the first book." Which made me felt silly. Then I read on.<br /><br /><strong>The Magician's Nephew</strong> was a del</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17970933628451423984">When I first grabbed the book, my thought was, "Oh my! There's a whole lot to read." As you can see, I have watched the Chronicles of Narnia movies 1 to 3 (The Lion, The Witch and The Wardrobe; Prince Caspian; The Voyage of The Dawn Treader) before reading the book itself, and it was so thick!. So when I came across the 1st book, I was puzzled. I thought, "oh, so The Lion, The Witch and The Wardrobe was not the first book." Which made me felt silly. Then I read on.<br /><br /><strong>The Magician's Nephew</strong> was a delight read! This was the origin of the realms of Narnia. And as I have known the story of the wardrobe as their gateway to Narnia, it was exciting to know where it all started. And my curiosity was satisfied in this episode. It was like being kid again and really being in this adventure.<br /><br />Then, this episode of the infamous <strong>The Lion, The Witch, and The Wardrobe</strong> was exactly as what the movie has reeled. So, while I was reading, it was confirming the story in my head as I have seen it. But it gave me a more learned sense why Aslan was someone to fear with by the enemies. So, I had admired Aslan more while reading on.<br /><br /><strong>The Horse and His Boy</strong> was something else. I enjoyed every bit of it. It's like learning moral values from your father through his story. How the fate has played its part. It's a very simple story but it has a great impact to me, it became my favorite book on all 7 books. In here, there were no sons of Adam nor daughters of Eve from our world. Just a story about a prince who got separated from his family because of the prophecy that he will unknowingly fulfill. (Of course with Aslan's grace.)<br /><br />The fourth book was turned into a film too...<strong>Prince Caspian</strong>. There were no love interest between Susan and Caspian in the book. The movie seemed to be grandeur than the book, nevertheless, I liked the book.<br /><br /><strong>The Voyage of the Dawn Treader</strong> was my favorite movie amongst the Narnia films. It was the most exciting of their adventures as there were so many things going on in this story. They traveled to seek for the 7 Lords of Narnia and what they found was one of a kind journey to the ends of the world. I like the character of Eustace when he became the dragon (oops, that's a spoiler!) and in here Caspian found his wife.<br /><br />Then, <strong>The Silver Chair</strong> was a different story where they embarked on a quests underworld. But before that, there were Gentle Giants who were not so gentle . Makes me recall the story about Jack and the Beanstalk. Exciting. Dangerous. When I started reading it, I didn't want to stop. It seemed on every page I was on the height of thrill.<br /><br />Finally, <strong>The Last Battle</strong>, the last book of the Chronicles of Narnia, was a wonderful conclusion. I was so dismayed with talking ape named Shift (who was the villain) but hey, it was his job to make the reader angry. It was so effective that I wanted to port to Narnia to give him a slap in his crumpled face (because he was an old ape). So, again, Eustace and Jill came to the rescue. I was not so sure how it would go really, I thought that no one from our world would come, but they did. I was glad. Then the seven Kings and Queens came~~and I won't spoil it. You read the book to know why they came and I promised a nice ending awaits you.<br /><br />While I was reading the last bit, I thought of the new heaven and earth that was promised to us by God. Because the story came to me like that, where everything is new and wonderful that you really can't describe it.<br /><br />Clive S. Lewis was a great story teller. It felt like he was just there telling the story to me. How he put it to words were so amazingly simple to understand, yet very detailed and witty. I gave 5 stars to all books, they deserve it!<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17970933628451423984">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer535207944">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3071698729567816384">Well ... what can I say? I feel ridiculous trying to write a review about a series of books that have been so close to my heart since I was a very young child. How can I truly express how great they are, and how personally life-changing they were to me? I can't. So I will just say ..<br /><br />If you haven't read these - read them. No matter who you are, or what age, or what type/genre of book you usually read - no one should go through life without having read this amazing series by one of the best author</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3071698729567816384">Well ... what can I say? I feel ridiculous trying to write a review about a series of books that have been so close to my heart since I was a very young child. How can I truly express how great they are, and how personally life-changing they were to me? I can't. So I will just say ..<br /><br />If you haven't read these - read them. No matter who you are, or what age, or what type/genre of book you usually read - no one should go through life without having read this amazing series by one of the best authors I know.<br /><br />Read them.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1300322988">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1300322988" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/13676925-bene" class="left imgcol" title="Bene"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1429899310p2/13676925.jpg" alt="Bene" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1300322988?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 07, 2015</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4221720778790307210"><br />"Un giorno sarai grande abbastanza da ricominciare a leggere le favole." <br />C.S. Lewis.<br /><br />Incredibile come questo libro mi catturi ogni volta, come se fosse la prima, come ogni volta mi commuova nei soliti punti, come non mi stanchi mai di rileggerlo<br />Il linguaggio è semplice e scorrevole ma tratteggiato con dettaglio.<br />La descrizione del paesaggio fiabesco è particolareggiata ma mai troppo lenta, poiché si alterna nei giusti momenti per dare spazio ai dialoghi e all'azione.<br />Quelle che potrebbero sembrare</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4221720778790307210"><br />"Un giorno sarai grande abbastanza da ricominciare a leggere le favole." <br />C.S. Lewis.<br /><br />Incredibile come questo libro mi catturi ogni volta, come se fosse la prima, come ogni volta mi commuova nei soliti punti, come non mi stanchi mai di rileggerlo<br />Il linguaggio è semplice e scorrevole ma tratteggiato con dettaglio.<br />La descrizione del paesaggio fiabesco è particolareggiata ma mai troppo lenta, poiché si alterna nei giusti momenti per dare spazio ai dialoghi e all'azione.<br />Quelle che potrebbero sembrare delle semplici fiabe per bambini si caricano invece di significati simbolici profondi che gradualmente il lettore può scorgere.<br />Una serie per chi desidera entrare in un mondo fantastico che non finisce mai di sorprendere; per chi, nonostante tutto, è sempre alla ricerca di valori e infine per chi, come da bambino, non ha mai smesso di sognare.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1284058217">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14673740198067499811"><u> <em>The Magician's Nephew Review</em> </u><br /><br /><strong>ACTUAL RATING: 4.5 stars</strong><br /><br />I read this book for the Mid-year #CRAMATHON 2015!<br /><br />This book was just what I needed. I've been feeling a bit of a slump coming on, and I was craving some fantasy, and this book was just perfect. So perfect that I almost read it in one sitting, then my mum told me to "go outside, for a change, get some air"<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436624113ra/15485714.gif" /><br /><br />I have only seen the movies for The Chronicles of Narnia (CoN), but I believe there is no film of "The Magician's Nephew". This book is the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14673740198067499811"><u>
  <em>The Magician's Nephew Review</em>
</u><br /><br /><strong>ACTUAL RATING: 4.5 stars</strong><br /><br />I read this book for the Mid-year #CRAMATHON 2015!<br /><br />This book was just what I needed. I've been feeling a bit of a slump coming on, and I was craving some fantasy, and this book was just perfect. So perfect that I almost read it in one sitting, then my mum told me to "go outside, for a change, get some air"<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436624113ra/15485714.gif" /><br /><br />I have only seen the movies for The Chronicles of Narnia (CoN), but I believe there is no film of "The Magician's Nephew". This book is the start of CoN, and it perfectly shows the creation of Narnia. <br /><br />I know Christianity is a major inspiration for this whole series, <strong>and this first book is not even subtle about it.</strong><br /><br />The concept of the Creation of the Earth, as depicted in the Bible, is very much translated almost word for word in the second half of this book (of course with slight plot changes and character changes, because I do believe that "God" was not a singing Lion, and I'm fairly certain you can't grow toffee trees...) This, however, did not bother me, as the elements of fantasy and magic is tossed in there to make a nice enjoyable story. When we first met Queen Jadis, I was hoping she would be kind and fair. But it turns out she's actually the White Witch in the rest of the series... So that's a nice little back story for her (I guess this whole book is a backstory really, considering it really <em>is</em> the creation of Narnia)<br /><br />Reading the first half of the book, I kept on asking myself if I liked it, and I kept on replying<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436624113ra/15485715.gif" /><br /><br />But it didn't take long (when Aslan arrived) when I did start to really enjoy it.<br /><br /><strong>Fab Aslan be like:</strong><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436624113ra/15485716.gif" /><br /><br />I have to end this review.<br /><br />I really enjoyed this book and I can't wait to read the next one!<br /><br />(Sorry, but this is my favourite gif omg)<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1436624113ra/15485717.gif" /><br /><br /><br /><u>
  <em>The Lion, The Witch, and The Wardrobe Review</em>
</u><br /><br /><strong>ACTUAL RATING: 3.75 STARS</strong><br /><br />I read this book for the #CRAMATHON 2015!<br /><br />This was a bit of a disappointment for me. After reading <em>The Magician's Nephew</em>, and loving it, I had high hopes for this story which I was already familiar with because I had seen the movie before (quite a few times actually). But I found the story quite slow, and when compared to the movie much less exciting and thrilling. <br /><br />Nevertheless, I do intend on continuing on with the series.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/162817164?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 22, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5304659-veronica" class="user" title="Veronica">Veronica</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5304659-veronica?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer162817164">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9503352810518078195">Who must a person be, to create a world where children, adults and aged people can live in? When I finished reading these series, I realized I might as well keep the books always at hand on my shelf, because I would reach out and read them constantly throughout the rest of my life. <br />And I guess that the best thing these books have taught me is that it's so easy to outgrow faith like you outgrow clothes once you leave your childhood behind. These stories, more than anything, encouraged me to keep</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9503352810518078195">Who must a person be, to create a world where children, adults and aged people can live in? When I finished reading these series, I realized I might as well keep the books always at hand on my shelf, because I would reach out and read them constantly throughout the rest of my life. <br />And I guess that the best thing these books have taught me is that it's so easy to outgrow faith like you outgrow clothes once you leave your childhood behind. These stories, more than anything, encouraged me to keep resizing that cloak of faith so that it fits until I stop growing and begin to shrink once more, and even then so that my faith keeps on growing forever! Like I heard someone say once, Narnia showed me that it's okay to leave childhood behind, but that we must never forget it. And for those who had a difficult childhood, like the Pevensie children in the middle of a World War, that there is always the loving watch of Someone who cares more than enough to show us a beautiful reality where the possibilities are endless; where freedom, love, and courage are just another way to live every single day.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_319179888">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/319179888" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7158234-katherine" class="left imgcol" title="Katherine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1323619018p2/7158234.jpg" alt="Katherine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/319179888?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 26, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7158234-katherine" class="user" title="Katherine">Katherine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10117970-the-chronicles-of-narnia" title="Paperback 9780007419" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7158234-katherine?shelf=all-time-faves" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-faves</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7158234-katherine?shelf=christian-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">christian-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7158234-katherine?shelf=christian-themes" class="actionLinkLite">christian-themes</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer319179888">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8128504483319490408">As a pre-teen/young teen I decided not to read these because they had to0 much magic. Yes, people-who-know-me, reel in shock! These weren't Christian enough for me! But now I love them... cue the quotes...<br /><br /><strong>"But it is not, as some people think, an allegory"</strong> (“Letter to Sophia Storr,” in The Collected Letters of C.S. Lewis, vol 3, 1113).<br /><br /><strong>"You are mistaken when you think that everything in the books ‘represents’ something in this world. Things do that in The Pilgrim’s Progress but I’m not writing in</strong></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8128504483319490408">As a pre-teen/young teen I decided not to read these because they had to0 much magic. Yes, people-who-know-me, reel in shock! These weren't Christian enough for me! But now I love them... cue the quotes...<br /><br /><strong>"But it is not, as some people think, an allegory"</strong> (“Letter to Sophia Storr,” in The Collected Letters of C.S. Lewis, vol 3, 1113).<br /><br /><strong>"You are mistaken when you think that everything in the books ‘represents’ something in this world. Things do that in The Pilgrim’s Progress but I’m not writing in that way"</strong> (Walter Hooper, Literary Criticism, 426).<br /><br />According to Lewis, an allegory “a composition (whether pictorial or literary) in which immaterial realities are represented by feigned physical objects, e.g. a pictured Cupid allegorically represents erotic love (which in reality is an experience, not an object occupying a given area of space) or, in Bunyan, a giant represents Despair” (“Letter to Mrs. Hook,” in The Collected Letters of C.S. Lewis, vol 3). <br /><br />However, just because they aren't Christian allegories doesn't mean they have no Christian meaning at all.<br /><br />Rather than allegory, Lewis calls the books a "supposal":<br /><br />"I did not say to myself ‘Let us represent Jesus as He really is in our world by a Lion in Narnia’: I said ‘Let us suppose that there were a land like Narnia and that the Son of God, as He became a Man in our world, became a Lion there, and then imagine what would have happened.’ If you think about it, you will see that it is quite a different thing." (Walter Hooper, Literary Criticism, 426)<br /><br />"If Aslan represented the immaterial Deity, he would be an allegorical figure. In reality however he is an invention giving an imaginary answer to the question, ‘What might Christ become like if there really were a world like Narnia and He chose to be incarnate and die and rise again in that world as He actually has done in ours." (“Letter to Mrs. Hook”)<br /><br />Personally, I like this view of the Narnia series much more than I do an allegorical view of it. It is nothing like other allegories I have read (namely <a title="Hinds' Feet on High Places by Hannah Hurnard" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/821056.Hinds__Feet_on_High_Places">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="Hinds' Feet on High Places by Hannah Hurnard" src="http://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1312284337s/821056.jpg" /></a> and <a title="The Pilgrim's Progress by John Bunyan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29797.The_Pilgrim_s_Progress">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="The Pilgrim's Progress by John Bunyan" src="http://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/books/1328865403s/29797.jpg" /></a>) as it is far too free with the representations and the inclusion of mythical creatures and magic. There are several elements in the books that don't work as allegory:<br /><br /> - Aslan: variously representing God, Jesus and Holy Spirit but never as the trinity.<br /> - Multiple other worlds: about as far from Biblical as you can get. If there were multiple worlds, according to Christianity, a saviour would have to go to each world, which means God would have multiple sons (which means no trinity). If he only chose to send one world a saviour, that would be unjust.<br /> - The various ways of getting in and out of Narnia: no Christian or Biblical precedent.<br /> - All the mythical creatures.<br /> - Father Christmas. Hello?!<br /><br />To conclude, I prefer the view of these books as fantasy stories with Christian elements far more than the common view of them as allegories. If you want an allegory go read Hind's Feet on High Places then come back and write me two lists: "Similarities" and "Differences". I'll give you one guess as to which list will be longer.<br /><br />I love these books, and so does my husband. We have an audio book version also which we used to listen to a lot, though sadly we have fallen out of the habit of listening to our various audio books. No doubt we will read these books to our children (or just stick on the audio version...) and so hopefully pass on a love of the series to them.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8128504483319490408">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer15673077495590342535">These dates are a guess, since I bought and read the book sometime around when the first movie came out. <br /><br />While I did like this bind up, there were some stories I liked more than others. <em>The Magicians Nephew</em> being my favorite. Then <em>The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> I enjoyed <em>Prince Caspian</em>, and <em>The Dawn Treader</em>. But after that the books kind of got 'meh' for me. Then <em>The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe</em> I enjoyed <em>Prince Caspian</em>, and <em>The Dawn Treader</em>. But after that the books kind of got 'meh' for me. The last one was really depressing and I actually kind of hated the ending to the series.<br /><br />These books are classics and even though I don't think they are as amazing as many, I still think people should pick these books up.</span>

Il ritorno dei 4 ragazzi a Narnia è spettacolare e Ripicì è uno spasso!<br />Ammetto però che nel film si son prese troppe licenze poetiche, tipo il ritorno della Strega Bianca o l'assalto al castello di Miraz... :-/<br />Il</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5679346639948999622"><strong>Riepilogo delle valutazioni dei libri:</strong><br />Il nipote del mago: 4. Il racconto della nascita di Narnia è emozionante! Lo adoro! &lt;3<br />Il leone, la strega e l'armadio: 5! Mi sono innamorata del libro dopo averlo visto al cinema: fantastico! :D<br />Il cavallo e il ragazzo: 4<br />Il principe Caspian: 5!!! Il ritorno dei 4 ragazzi a Narnia è spettacolare e Ripicì è uno spasso!<br />Ammetto però che nel film si son prese troppe licenze poetiche, tipo il ritorno della Strega Bianca o l'assalto al castello di Miraz... :-/<br />Il viaggio del veliero: 5. L'unico libro di cui non ho ancora visto la trasposizione cinematografica. Dovrò rimediare, prima o poi... :)<br />La sedia d'argento: 4 stelle, che in realtà è un 4 meno meno. Ancora non capisco il senso di questo libro: se Lewis non l'avesse scritto forse avrebbe fatto meglio... :-/<br />L'ultima battaglia: 5 stelle, o meglio 5 meno meno.<br />L'autore avrebbe dovuto dare più importanza alla battaglia dell'Ultimo Re di Narnia, invece sembra quasi che non vedesse l'ora di richiamare dalla Terra i vecchi Re e Regine del passato e di occuparsi di loro e della rinascita di Narnia dopo l'Apocalisse...<br /><br />In compenso il libro tiene la valutazione che gli diedi in passato, quando lo lessi per la prima volta, una decina di anni fa. E' uno dei fantasy che più adoro: so che non ci si dovrebbe innamorare di un libro solo perché si è vista la trasposizione al cinema (che può essere pure differente dal libro stesso) ma a me è successo così: appena lo vidi in libreria, lo presi e lo lessi d'un fiato. E' un "mattonazzo" che ti conquista, che ti afferra e non riesci a smettere di leggere. E per favore, non chiamatela "brutta copia del Signore degli Anelli": è vero che Tolkien e Lewis erano buoni amici, ma hanno scritto opere completamente differenti, sia come argomenti che come stile di scrittura.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="5679346639948999622">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_474040199">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/474040199" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/12709025-luke" class="left imgcol" title="Luke"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1358009679p2/12709025.jpg" alt="Luke" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/474040199?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 23, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/12709025-luke" class="user" title="Luke">Luke</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a onclick="Effect.toggle('reviewTextContainer474040199'); return false;" href="#">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span style="display:none" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer474040199">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8732731778204059663">This week I read the chronicles of Narnia by C.S. Lewis. It was a very interesting book. C.S. Lewis wrote it to help children understand Christianity. It is actually a series of seven books. In the first book, The Magician's Nephew, two children go into several different dimensions, including Narnia, and Narnia is created by Aslan, the lion. The books never say who or what Aslan is, why he is omnipotent, why though he is omnipotent, he didn't stop the White WItch until the four Pevensie children</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8732731778204059663">This week I read the chronicles of Narnia by C.S. Lewis. It was a very interesting book. C.S. Lewis wrote it to help children understand Christianity. It is actually a series of seven books. In the first book, The Magician's Nephew, two children go into several different dimensions, including Narnia, and Narnia is created by Aslan, the lion. The books never say who or what Aslan is, why he is omnipotent, why though he is omnipotent, he didn't stop the White WItch until the four Pevensie children got there, or how he created Narnia. (I believe he represents Jesus) Also, a witch follows them from her shattered dimension into Narnia, and later becomes the White Witch in he second book. In the second book, the four Pevensie siblings, Peter, Susan, Edmund, and Lucy, get into Narnia, years later, through a wardrobe in one of the first two children's (Who is now a extremely old professor" house. They enter Narnia, where it is always winter yet never christmas, due to the White Witch's spell. The White Witch tried to kill them, due to a prophecy that when four children sit upon the thrones, she will be vanquished. Aslan reappears and, after a battle, slays the White Witch. In the third book, The Horse and His Boy, it tells the tale of Shasta, a boy in the kingdom of Calormen, a large desert country far to the south of Narnia, which is always either conquering more land or keeping down rebellions. Shasta runs away with a talking horse, and turns out to be the crown prince of Archenland, who was kidnapped many years ago. He thwarts a Calormene invasion of Archenland by the crown prince, Rabadash. The fourth book, Prince Caspian, takes place one year later on earth, but one thousand three hundred years later in Narnia, when the Pevensie children re-arrive in Narnia. Prince Caspian X, the rightful heir to Narnia, is being hunted by his uncle, King Miraz, who obtained the throne by killing his own brother, King Caspian IX. Narnia breaks into civil war, with many of the humans supporting Miraz, and the mythical characters supporting Prince Caspian. Peter challenges Miraz to a duel and when Miraz loses and is knocked down, his treacherous lords, Lord Sopespian and Lord Glozelle, stab Miraz in the back, killing him. The war then ends and Aslan sends the Pevensie's back to the real world. In the fifth book, The Voyage of the Dawn Treader, Prince Caspian, now King Caspian, is going on a voyage to find the seven lords who were his fathers friends and were sent on quests across the sea by Miraz. The two youngest Pevensie children, Edmund and Lucy are staying with their cousins when, through a picture in the guest bedroom, they and their cousin are sucked into Narnia onto Caspian's boat, the Dawn Treader. The first place they sail is to the Lone Islands, where slave trade flourishes, there they meet Lord Bern, one of the seven lords. Caspian replaces the corrupt governor with the lord. The second place they stop is a unnamed island with a large mountain. They stop for repairs on a beach and their bratty cousin, Eustace, climbed the mountain and finds a dead dragon and his cave. Eustace falls asleep and turns into a dragon, before Aslan turns him back. As a dragon, he put on a bracelet that had belonged to Lord Octesian, they speculate that either he was the dead dragon, or he was eaten by it. The third island is one with a fountain that turns everything into gold. In it, they find Lord Lord Restimar's golden corpse. He had dived into the fountain and been killed and transmuted into gold. The fourth island is one where one re-experiences their worst nightmares. They rescue Lord Rhoop, who has had his mind shattered. The last island is one where the fallen star Ramandu lives. The last three lords, Lords Mavramorn, Revilian and Argoz are their in a deep sleep that will only broken if they get to the end of the world and leave sombody behind. They do so and return to earth. The sixth book, the silver chair tells of Eustace and his friend jill. King Caspian's son was kidnapped ten years ago by a witch. They go below Narnia to try and save him. They free him and he kills the witch. In the seventh and last book, it is much like judgement day. An evil ape and a crowd of unbelievers in Aslan rise up and Aslan ends the world, bringing the worthy into the true Narnia, which I think represents heaven. My favorite book was the Magician's Nephew, because it was very interesting and answered many of my questions about the series. and my favorite line is "It is always winter, yet never christmas," as it is really humorous.<br /><br />I connected with this book because I am really interested in fantasy and I learned about some books have many references to the bible, such as in the Voyage of the Dawn Treader, Aslan saying, "You will learn to call me by another name in your world." I think he was talking about Jesus. Overall, I loved this book and would recommend it to anyone. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8732731778204059663">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis">About C.S. Lewis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/1069006.C_S_Lewis"><img alt="1069006" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1367519078p2/1069006.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer14236027509543725875">CLIVE STAPLES LEWIS (1898–1963) was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14236027509543725875">CLIVE STAPLES LEWIS (1898–1963) was one of the intellectual giants of the twentieth century and arguably one of the most influential writers of his day. He was a Fellow and Tutor in English Literature at Oxford University until 1954. He was unanimously elected to the Chair of Medieval and Renaissance Literature at Cambridge University, a position he held until his retirement. He wrote more than thirty books, allowing him to reach a vast audience, and his works continue to attract thousands of new readers every year. His most distinguished and popular accomplishments include <em>Mere Christianity</em>, <em>Out of the Silent Planet</em>, <em>The Great Divorce</em>, <em>The Screwtape Letters</em>, and the universally acknowledged classics <em>The Chronicles of Narnia</em>. To date, the Narnia books have sold over 100 million copies and been transformed into three major motion pictures.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14236027509543725875">...more</a>

    </div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/781271-the-chronicles-of-narnia">Quotes from The Chronicles of...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“Peter did not feel very brave; indeed, he felt he was going to be sick. But that made no difference to what he had to do.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/56718-peter-did-not-feel-very-brave-indeed-he-felt-he" class="actionLinkLite">261 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“But, first, remember, remember, remember the signs. Say them to yourself when you wake in the morning and when you lie down at night, and when you wake in the middle of the night. And whatever strange things may happen to you, let nothing turn your mind from following the signs. And secondly, I give you a warning. Here on the mountain I have spoken to you clearly: I will not often do so down in Narnia. Here on the mountain, the air is clear and your mind is clear; as you drop down into Narnia, the air will thicken. Take great care that it does not confuse your mind. And the signs which you have learned here will not look at all as you expect them to look, when you meet them there. That is why it is so important to know them by heart and pay no attention to appearances. Remember the signs and believe the signs. Nothing else matters.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/66683-but-first-remember-remember-remember-the-signs-say-them-to" class="actionLinkLite">242 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
        <a href="/work/quotes/781271-the-chronicles-of-narnia" style="float: right" class="actionLink">More quotes…</a>
